# Explanation of the Visualizations
The first graph is a bar graph from the OECD website that looks at the government debt to GDP ratio from 1996 - 2018 (or latest available) for 35 countries . This ratio measures the gross debt of the general government as a percentage of GDP. The OECD website says that looking at this ratio year over year can help assess the impact of previous government deficit. The use of a bar graph in this instance allows audiences to instantly compare the levels of government debt between countries. However, there is no time-component in this graph which makes it difficult to visualize change over time. The interactive aspect allows users to get a more accurate picture of the debt to GDP ratio. 

The second graph is a beeswarm diagram that utilizes size to differentiate changes in the ratio over time and by location. The size of the dot represents the magnitude of the ratio. This graph not only allows audiences to visualize which years have relatively high debt ratios but it also shows how many data points there are for each country. It clearly shows that there are a lack of values for certain countries during certain years which is interesting. Unfortunately this graph is not intuitive. Looking at the size of the bubbles allows us to see only whether debt ratio has increased or decreased relatively, it is very difficult to see with any accuracy what the actual value of those numbers are. 

The final graph is the one I created which is a scatterplot that plots the debt ratio over time by country with different colors used to represent different countries. I chose a scatterplot because it is the best in identifying trends. Looking at this graph instantly gives insight into whether the debt ratio has been systematically increasing for countries. With 35 countries it was difficult to make color choices. I chose black for the United States so that it would stand out compared to the rest of the colors.
